[4:0:344,458] agricultural issues, and services to veterans that  

[4:1:344,468] are not easily consolidated into other oversight  

[4:2:344,478] areas. Additionally, this Section includes issues  

[4:3:344,488] that are statewide in nature such as lease/revenue  

[4:4:344,498] issues, bonds, and local government issues.

[1:0:134,39] 8120   Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training  

[2:0:66,61] The Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training (POST)   is responsible for raising the competence level of law  

[2:1:66,71] enforcement officers in California by establishing minimum selection and training standards, improving management practices,  

[2:2:66,81] and providing financial assistance to local agencies relating to the training of law enforcement officers. POST promotes training  

[2:3:66,91] to improve quality, integrity, accountability, and cooperation; encourages new ideas; explores and uses appropriate  

[2:4:66,101] technologies; and delivers relevant, client-based programs and services.

[1:0:446,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[2:0:99,32] 8120   Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] 6500 - STANDARDS  

[4:0:66,89] The Standards Program establishes job-related selection and training standards for peace officers and dispatchers. It also  

[4:1:66,99] provides management consultation to local law enforcement agencies. Activities include development of examinations and  

[4:2:66,109] counseling local law enforcement agencies on ways to improve management practices.  

[5:0:66,130] The Standards Program conducts applied research in the areas of peace officer selection and training, operational procedures  

[5:1:66,140] and program evaluation to meet statutory requirements and to provide management guidance to local law enforcement  

[5:2:66,150] agencies. It also facilitates the development and implementation of new programs for local agencies by serving as a  

[5:3:66,160] clearinghouse of successful program information. Finally, it conducts accreditation and peace officer feasibility studies.  

[5:4:66,178] 6505 - TRAINING  

[6:0:66,199] The Training Program increases the competence of law enforcement personnel by developing and certifying courses that meet  

[6:1:66,209] identified training needs. This activity requires scheduling and quality control of such courses and assisting law enforcement  

[6:2:66,219] agencies in presenting necessary training and career development programs to their officers. The Commission assesses  

[6:3:66,229] training on a continuing basis to ensure that evolving training needs are met. The curricula cover a wide variety of topics  

[6:4:66,239] necessary to satisfy statutory and regulatory mandates, maintain competence in police work, and address the training needs of  

[6:5:66,250] law enforcement agency personnel. Curricula content is updated regularly. The Training Program also presents advanced  

[6:6:66,260] training for law enforcement supervisors and executives through its Command College and the Supervisory Leadership  

[6:7:66,270] Institute, and for trainers through the Basic Academy Instructor Certificate Program and the Instructor Development Institute.  

[6:8:66,280] Other specialty programs include the Institute of Criminal Investigation.  

[7:0:66,301] Job-related selection and training standards for peace officers and dispatchers, established by the Standards Program, are  

[7:1:66,311] enforced through inspections of local agencies receiving state aid to ensure they are adhering to minimum state standards.  

[7:2:66,328] 6510 - PEACE OFFICER TRAINING  

[8:0:66,349] The Peace Officer Training Program provides financial assistance to participating jurisdictions for instructional costs associated  

[8:1:66,359] with selected training courses. Funding is also provided for the cost of student travel and per diem expenses associated with  

[8:2:66,369] training presentations to enable line officers to receive in-service training in areas of critical need. To encourage and assist local  

[8:3:66,379] law enforcement agencies to meet and maintain minimum standards in the selection and training of law enforcement officers,  

[8:4:66,390] the Commission provides financial assistance to all 58 counties, approximately 346 cities, and numerous specialized districts  

[8:5:66,400] and local agencies which have agreed to meet the Commission's standards.  

[8:6:66,418] 6515 - POST ADMINISTRATION  

[9:0:66,438] The   objective of the California Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training is to be the leading organization in  

[9:1:66,448] establishing professional standards, assuring top-quality training, and providing effective services to develop and support  

[9:2:66,458] California law enforcement personnel.   California POST utilizes the following   eight bureaus to ensure uniform standards and  

[9:3:66,469] compliance are applied to over 600 law enforcement agencies and 40 police academies statewide: Basic Training;  

[9:4:66,479] Management Counseling and   Projects;   Strategic Communications and Research; Learning Technology Resources; Training  

[9:5:66,489] Program Services; Training Delivery and Compliance; Computer Services; and Administrative Services.

[9:0:225,387] 8140   State Public Defender

[9:2:66,419] following a judgment of death.   Furthermore, the   Office of the State Public Defender is also mandated to provide assistance and

[9:4:66,439] engage in related efforts for the purpose of improving indigent defense.

[6:0:66,290] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:1:66,309] 6530 - STATE PUBLIC DEFENDER  

[7:0:66,330] The objective of the Office of the State Public Defender, upon assignment of cases exclusively from the California Supreme  

[7:1:66,340] Court, is to provide legal services in capital appeals to persons who do not have the financial means to employ private counsel.  

[7:2:66,350] The Office of the State Public Defender is also mandated to provide assistance and training to public defender offices,  

[7:3:66,360] appointed private counsel, and counsel appointed to represent indigent juveniles, and to engage in related efforts for the  

[7:4:66,371] purpose of improving indigent defense. The State Public Defender is headquartered in Oakland, and has a regional office in  

[7:5:66,381] Sacramento.

[11:0:222,550] 8260   California Arts Council  

[11:1:66,572] The California Arts Council (Council) consists of eleven members, nine appointed by the Governor and one each appointed by  

[11:2:66,582] the President pro Tempore of the Senate and the Speaker of the Assembly. The Council establishes general policy and  

[11:3:66,592] approves program allocations.  

[12:0:66,612] The Council recognizes that the Arts are essential for the cultural, educational, social and economic development of California.  

[12:1:66,623] The Council seeks to further its mandates and services to the public through the development of partnerships with the public  

[12:2:66,633] and private sectors and by providing support to the state's non-profit arts and cultural community, which are broad-based and  

[12:3:66,643] extended across the state from its largest metropolitan areas to its most rural areas.  

[13:0:66,663] The Council is statutorily required to:  

[13:1:65,681] • Encourage artistic awareness, participation, and expression among the citizens of California.  

[13:2:65,697] • Help independent local groups develop their own arts programs.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[11:0:66,469] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[11:1:65,488] •  California Creative Corps Pilot Program— The Budget includes $60 million one-time General Fund in 2021-22, to be spent  

[11:2:76,498] over three years, to implement the California Creative Corps Pilot Program. This program will support artists and local art  

[11:3:76,509] organizations with a focus on art campaigns that bring awareness to stopping the spread of COVID-19, water and energy  

[11:4:76,519] conservation, emergency preparedness, response and recovery, civic engagement, and social justice. The Arts Council will  

[11:5:76,529] provide grants in every county and prioritize grants in zip codes in the lowest quartile of the California Healthy Places Index.  

[11:6:65,545] •  Creative Youth Development Grant Programs— The Budget includes $40 million one-time General Fund in 2021-22, to be  

[11:7:76,555] spent over three years, to support the Arts Council’s existing Creative Youth Development programs. The Arts Council will  

[11:8:76,566] use partnerships between community-based organizations, educators, and local artists to expand participation in these  

[11:9:76,576] programs statewide.

[5:0:154,171] 8385   California Citizens Compensation Commission  

[5:1:66,192] The California Citizens Compensation Commission is responsible for setting the salaries and benefits for the Governor,  

[5:2:66,202] Lieutenant Governor, State Legislators, Attorney General, Secretary of State, Controller, Treasurer, Superintendent of Public  

[5:3:66,212] Instruction, Insurance Commissioner, and Board of Equalization members.  

[5:4:66,230] The seven-member Commission meets annually, no later than June 30, to determine if any changes should be made to the  

[5:5:66,240] salaries and benefits of the state's elected officials. The decisions of the Commission become effective the first Monday in  

[5:6:66,251] December following the annual meeting.  

[6:0:66,268] The members of the Commission are appointed by the Governor. Three members represent the public, two members represent  

[6:1:66,278] the business community, and two members represent labor organizations. Each member serves a six-year term and the terms  

[6:2:66,289] are staggered.  

[7:0:66,306] The Commission's budget provides for travel expenses and stipends for the annual meeting. Department of Human Resources  

[7:1:66,317] staff provide administrative support to the Commission using its existing resources.

[8:0:183,320] 8420   State Compensation Insurance Fund  

[9:0:66,342] State Compensation Insurance Fund (State Fund) is a public enterprise fund established through legislation that was originally  

[9:1:66,352] enacted in 1913. State Fund is governed by a board of directors with authority comparable to that of the governing body of a  

[9:2:66,362] private insurance carrier. State Fund has its own separate budget process that is presented to its board of directors for  

[9:3:66,372] approval.  

[10:0:66,393] State Fund's purpose is to provide fairly priced workers' compensation insurance, make California workplaces safe, restore  

[10:1:66,403] injured workers, and operate as the continuously available market for workers' compensation insurance for employers located  

[10:2:66,413] in California. Pursuant to the Insurance Code, State Fund is to be self-supporting with revenues from premiums on policies  

[10:3:66,423] written and from investment income. State Fund does not receive any financial support from the State of California, and the  

[10:4:66,434] state is not liable for any obligations of State Fund. Almost all of State Fund's employees are hired through the California civil  

[10:5:66,444] service process and are actually employees of the state.  

[11:0:66,464] State Fund is considered a related organization to the State of California because State Fund was created by the state and  

[11:1:66,474] because the Governor appoints a voting majority of State Fund's board of directors. However, the State of California is not  

[11:2:66,485] financially liable or responsible for the operations or solvency of State Fund.  

[12:0:66,505] Most of the state agencies and departments of the State of California are legally uninsured for workers' compensation coverage  

[12:1:66,515] and benefits for injured employees. Separate from its issuance of workers' compensation insurance policies to private  

[12:2:66,525] employers, State Fund has been engaged by the California Department of Human Resources to provide workers'  

[12:3:66,536] compensation claims administration services for these legally uninsured departments, agencies, boards, commissions, and  

[12:4:66,546] other subdivisions of the State of California under a Master Services Agreement. Under this agreement, the state provides a  

[12:5:66,556] deposit out of which State Fund pays compensation benefits to injured state workers and medical benefits to health providers,  

[12:6:66,566] and State Fund is reimbursed by state agencies for benefit amounts paid plus a service fee equal to the cost of these services.  

[13:0:66,587] For informational purposes only, the table further below   provides summary data on actual and estimated workers'  

[13:1:66,597] compensation claims costs paid by state agencies. All workers' compensation costs are included in the individual budgets of  

[13:2:66,607] the responsible state agencies. Costs also include administrative charges under the Master Services Agreement.

[4:0:184,131] 8570   Department of Food and Agriculture  

[5:0:66,153] The California Department of Food and Agriculture serves the citizens of California by promoting and protecting a safe, healthy  

[5:1:66,163] food supply; and enhancing local and global agricultural trade, through efficient management, innovation, and sound science,  

[5:2:66,173] with a commitment to environmental stewardship.  

[6:0:66,194] The goals of the California Department of Food and Agriculture are to:  

[6:1:65,211] • Promote and protect the diverse local and global marketability of the California agricultural brand, which represents superior  

[6:2:76,221] quality, value, and safety.  

[7:0:65,238] • Optimize fiscal resources through collaboration, innovation, and process improvements.  

[8:0:65,254] • Connect rural and urban communities by supporting and participating in educational programs that emphasize a mutual  

[8:1:76,264] appreciation of the value of diverse food and agricultural production systems.  

[8:2:65,280] • Improve regulatory efficiency through proactive coordination with stakeholders.  

[8:3:65,296] • Invest in employee development and succession planning efforts.  

[9:0:66,314] Because the California Department of Food and Agriculture's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the  

[9:1:66,324] Department has a capital outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see  

[9:2:66,335] "Infrastructure Overview."

[16:0:66,587] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[16:1:65,607] • Fairground and Community Resilience Centers—The Budget includes $150 million General Fund one-time to support the  

[16:2:76,617] development and enhancement of community resilience centers. This funding is available to improve both local fairground  

[16:3:76,627] and other community facilities to enhance the state’s emergency preparedness capabilities, particularly in response to  

[16:4:76,637] climate change.  

[17:0:65,653] • Transfer Cannabis Resources to the Department of Cannabis Control—The Budget includes a shift of $64.1 million  

[17:1:76,664] Cannabis Control Fund and 155 positions to the new Department of Cannabis Control.  

[17:2:65,680] • Fairgrounds Operational Support—The Budget includes $50 million General Fund to continue supporting fairground  

[17:3:76,690] operational costs while the state continues to evaluate its relationship with fairs.  

[18:0:65,706] • State Water Efficiency and Enhancement Program Grants—The Budget includes $40 million General Fund in 2021-22, for

[3:0:76,69] grants that support agricultural water efficiency improvements, with a focus on depleted groundwater basins.  

[3:1:65,85] • Insect DNA Barcode Library—The Budget includes $11.5 million General Fund one-time to establish an Insect DNA Barcode  

[3:2:76,95] Library. This funding will support barcoding and preserving existing specimens, securing new specimens from priority  

[3:3:76,105] locations such as the Central Valley, expanding opportunities via professional and informal training, and delivering data to  

[3:4:76,115] support the state’s biodiversity actions.

[4:1:76,142] robust inspection program for the enforcement of the Food Safety Modernization Act, Produce Safety Rule in California.  

[4:2:65,158] • Bee Safe Program—The Budget includes $2.1 million General Fund one-time to continue operating a statewide Bee Safe

[4:4:65,184] • Pet Lover's Specialized License Plate Grant Program (SB 673)—The Budget includes $650,000 Special License Plate Fund

[5:0:65,221] • Agricultural and Rural Economic Advisor—The Budget includes $199,000 General Fund and one permanent position to  

[5:1:76,231] establish an agricultural and rural economic advisor to assist with economic recovery in the agricultural sector.  

[5:2:65,247] • Healthy Soils Program—The Budget includes $50 million one-time General Fund to provide grants for on-farm conservation  

[5:3:76,258] management practices designed to sequester carbon within the soil.  

[6:0:65,274] • Integrated Pest Management (IPM) Programs—The Budget includes $9.8 million one-time General Fund in 2021-22 and  

[6:1:76,284] $7.8 million in 2022-23, for grants for IPM research and education. This includes funding for the Biologically Integrated  

[6:2:76,294] Farming Systems grant program, proactive IPM and Interregional Research Project No. 4 research, and cooperative  

[6:3:76,304] agreements with California State University Agricultural Research Institute and the University of California Agriculture and  

[6:4:76,315] Natural Resources to expand research, education, and extension capacity for IPM practices.  

[6:5:65,331] • Technical Assistance Program for Underserved Farmers—The Budget includes $5.4 million one-time General Fund to  

[6:6:76,341] support technical assistance providers and small, mid-sized, and underserved farmers. These resources help farmers with  

[6:7:76,351] business planning, navigating regulatory compliance, and accessing additional state and federal funds.

[4:0:66,324] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:0:66,343] 6570 - AGRICULTURAL PLANT AND ANIMAL HEALTH; PEST PREVENTION; FOOD SAFETY SERVICES  

[6:0:66,364] The objective of this program is to prevent the introduction and establishment of serious plant and animal pests and diseases to  

[6:1:66,374] California, promote judicious antimicrobial use and stewardship   and protect the safety of California's dairy, eggs and meat  

[6:2:66,384] products that are exempt from federal inspection. In particular, the program is focused on pests and diseases that can: (1) be  

[6:3:66,394] transmitted to humans, (2) inflict catastrophic financial loss on California's farmers, ranchers, and associated businesses,  

[6:4:66,405] (3) have severe negative impacts on the environment, or (4) adversely affect the supply of agricultural products to the  

[6:5:66,415] consumer.  

[7:0:66,432] 6575 - MARKETING; COMMODITIES AND AGRICULTURAL SERVICES  

[8:0:66,453] California agriculture produces over 400 different crops, which enter state, national, and international commerce. The  

[8:1:66,463] objectives of this program are to assure orderly domestic and international marketing of safe and quality agricultural  

[8:2:66,473] commodities, promote consumer protection, increase food access, ensure fair pricing practices, oversee industry-supported  

[8:3:66,483] grading services, and maintain standards of measurement which provide a basis of value comparison, fair competition in the  

[8:4:66,494] marketplace, and establish quality standards for conventional and alternative fuels and automotive products.  

[8:5:66,511] This program also provides licenses to eligible California cannabis cultivators and maintains a statewide cannabis tracking  

[8:6:66,522] system. The cannabis program is proposed to be consolidated with other cannabis licensing programs under a new  

[8:7:66,532] Department of Cannabis Control beginning 2021-22.  

[9:0:66,552] Additionally, this program provides support to governmental agencies that work to protect the nation's food supply and the  

[9:1:66,562] environment by monitoring for chemical contaminants.  

[10:0:66,580] 6580 - ASSISTANCE TO FAIRS AND COUNTY AGRICULTURAL ACTIVITIES  

[11:0:66,600] This program provides fiscal and policy oversight to the network of California fairs.  

[12:0:66,621] The State has a network of 79 fairs including county fairs, citrus fruit fairs, District Agricultural Associations and the California  

[12:1:66,631] State Fair (an independent state agency). State oversight of these local fairs includes attendance of board meetings and  

[12:2:66,641] periodic financial reviews and audits.  

[12:3:66,659] 6590 - GENERAL AGRICULTURAL ACTIVITIES  

[13:0:66,679] This program provides fiscal and policy oversight   for federal grants that promote California agriculture, and for   CDFA  

[13:1:66,690] Greenhouse Gas Reduction Program activities   that are designed to reduce greenhouse gas emissions in agriculture.  

[14:0:66,710] In addition, this program serves as the central point of contact for logistical coordination of all departmental resources, provides  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[4:0:66,131] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[5:0:66,150] The California Department of Food and Agriculture's (CDFA) facilities support its operations serving the citizens of California by  

[5:1:66,161] promoting and protecting a safe and healthy food supply and enhancing local and global agricultural trade through efficient  

[5:2:66,171] management, innovation, and sound science, with a commitment to environmental stewardship. These facilities are in various  

[5:3:66,181] locations in Arizona, Hawaii, and throughout California. The CDFA rents or owns approximately 710,000 square feet of  

[5:4:66,191] facilities, including 41 field offices, 11 laboratories, 16 border protection stations, 9 employee residences, and a headquarters in  

[5:5:66,201] Sacramento.

[6:0:185,228] 8620   Fair Political Practices Commission  

[6:1:66,249] The Fair Political Practices Commission has primary responsibility for the impartial administration, implementation, and  

[6:2:66,259] enforcement of the Political Reform Act of 1974, as amended by the voters and Legislature. The purpose of the Act is to restore  

[6:3:66,269] confidence in governmental processes. The major objectives of the Commission are to:  

[6:4:65,287] • Provide education about the Act and its requirements to the public and the regulated community including public officials,  

[6:5:76,297] candidates, and lobbyists, and assist with compliance.  

[7:0:65,314] • Ensure that election campaign contribution and expenditure data is fully and accurately disclosed so that the voters may be  

[7:1:76,324] fully informed.  

[8:0:65,340] • Enforce the provisions of the Act and regulations fairly and with due process.  

[8:1:65,356] • Regulate the activities of lobbyists and disclose their finances.  

[9:0:65,372] • Provide for the disclosure of assets and income of public officials, which may affect their official actions, to avoid conflicts of  

[9:1:76,383] interest or appearances of impropriety.

[10:0:208,537] 8660   Public Utilities Commission  

[10:1:66,558] The California Public Utilities Commission (PUC) regulates critical and essential services such as privately-owned  

[10:2:66,568] telecommunications, electric—including utility wildfire safety—natural gas, and water companies, in addition to overseeing  

[10:3:66,579] railroad/rail transit and transportation companies. The PUC is the only agency in the state charged with protecting private utility  

[10:4:66,589] consumers. As such, the PUC regulates services and utilities, protects consumers, safeguards the environment, and assures  

[10:5:66,599] Californians' access to safe and reliable utility infrastructure and services.

[5:0:66,614] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,633] 6680 - REGULATION OF UTILITIES  

[5:2:66,651] Californians spend more than $50 billion annually for services from industries regulated by the PUC. This includes six electricity  

[5:3:66,661] utilities, 1,012 telecommunications carriers, 120 water and sewer utilities, five natural gas utilities with approximately 10.8  

[5:4:66,671] million customers, four gas facility owners, and more than 3,155 small mobile home park and propane operators.  

[5:5:66,689] The PUC oversees the safety of electric, communications, natural gas, and propane gas utility systems. Safety functions  

[5:6:66,699] include both backward-looking investigations and analysis of safety incidents and utility performance, and forward-looking risk  

[5:7:66,710] analytics to mitigate emerging threats and identify necessary regulatory rule reform. The PUC performs operation and  

[6:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 46 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — GG   44 

[1:0:446,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[2:0:173,32] 8660   Public Utilities Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] maintenance audits, outage inspections, mitigation reviews, and investigations of incidents at electric generation facilities and  

[3:1:66,79] coordinates closely with state public safety agencies to further resilience initiatives. The PUC’s safety oversight includes  

[3:2:66,89] enforcing public utility wildfire safety and Public Safety Power Shutoffs (PSPS) violations. It functions as the lead investigator  

[3:3:66,99] for utility incidents relating to wildfire events and recommends policy to strengthen PSPS guidelines. In addition to the PUC  

[3:4:66,109] opening formal investigations, the PUC imposes fines for natural gas, electric infrastructure, and wildfire safety-related  

[3:5:66,120] violations.  

[4:0:66,137] The PUC conducts reviews of utility operations every four years to determine just and reasonable rates for energy services.  

[4:1:66,148] The PUC provides guidelines for investor-owned utilities—and to a lesser extent, as directed by statute, an increasing number  

[4:2:66,158] of Community Choice Aggregators, Energy Service Providers, and Core Transport Agents—to follow when purchasing  

[4:3:66,168] electricity and natural gas on behalf of their customers to ensure that sufficient amounts of electricity are procured when and  

[4:4:66,178] where needed in the state.  

[5:0:66,196] Environmental protection and the impact of climate change are critical factors in PUC regulation. The PUC is charged with  

[5:1:66,206] implementing and overseeing key components of Chapter 547, Statutes of 2015 (SB 350); Chapter 312, Statutes of 2018 (SB  

[5:2:66,216] 100); and other climate change legislation, including promoting ratepayer-funded energy efficiency programs, Integrated  

[5:3:66,226] Resource Planning, the state's mandated renewable energy programs, programs for disadvantaged communities, electric  

[5:4:66,237] vehicle programs, and other clean energy programs.  

[6:0:66,254] The PUC oversees programs and regulations to promote both natural gas and electricity reliability and resilience.  

[7:0:66,272] The PUC establishes requirements for energy programs for low-income ratepayers, including programs providing rate  

[7:1:66,282] discounts, financial assistance with energy bills, and the Energy Savings Assistance Program, which provides no-cost  

[7:2:66,292] weatherization services to customers who meet low-income eligibility criteria.  

[8:0:66,310] The PUC conducts and manages environmental reviews pursuant to the California Environmental Quality Act for transmission,  

[8:1:66,320] telecommunications, and other infrastructure projects.  

[9:0:66,338] The PUC develops and implements policies for the rapidly changing communications and broadband markets, including  

[9:1:66,348] monitoring competitive markets for proper operation, enforcing carrier service standards for telecommunication services,  

[9:2:66,358] regulating rural telecommunication rates, protecting consumers against carrier abuses and fraud by applying licensing  

[9:3:66,369] requirements and rules for offering and terminating telecommunication services, and managing efficient deployment of area  

[9:4:66,379] codes and telephone numbers.  

[10:0:66,397] The PUC is responsible for ensuring that California's investor-owned water utilities deliver clean, safe, and reliable water to  

[10:1:66,407] their customers at reasonable rates. The PUC's water utility work includes investigating water and sewer service quality,  

[10:2:66,417] promoting water conservation and metering, improving low-income programs, analyzing and processing rate change requests,  

[10:3:66,427] and tracking and certifying compliance with PUC requirements.  

[11:0:66,445] The Public Advisor's Office provides procedural information, advice, and assistance to individuals and groups interested in  

[11:1:66,455] participating in PUC proceedings and advises the Commission on procedural matters related to public participation in  

[11:2:66,465] proceedings.  

[12:0:66,483] The Consumer Affairs Branch (CAB) responds to questions regarding utility service and bills and assists consumers who have  

[12:1:66,493] unresolved disputes with regulated utilities through an informal process. CAB also provides data on the consumer protection  

[12:2:66,503] issues it handles to stakeholders and decision makers to inform policy making. A Supplier Diversity program promotes and  

[12:3:66,514] monitors supplier diversity in procurement by utilities and oversees a certification clearinghouse.  

[13:0:66,531] 6685 - UNIVERSAL SERVICE TELECOMMUNICATIONS PROGRAMS 

[14:0:66,549] The PUC oversees telecommunication-based universal service programs (Universal Lifeline Telephone Service Trust Fund,  

[14:1:66,559] California Teleconnect Fund, Deaf and Disabled Telecommunications Program, California Advanced Services Fund, and two  

[14:2:66,569] California High Cost Funds), all of which provide needed services to consumers and communities. The objectives of these  

[14:3:66,580] "universal service" programs include: (1) ensuring basic telephone service is available to all Californians; (2) improving access  

[14:4:66,590] to broadband technology to bridge the digital divide; and (3) ensuring consumers have access to sufficient information to make  

[14:5:66,600] informed choices about basic service and universal lifeline telephone services.  

[15:0:66,620] The California LifeLine Program provides discounted basic residential wireless and wireline telephone service to qualified low-

[15:1:66,630] income households. The California Teleconnect Fund Program provides discounted telecommunication services to qualifying  

[15:2:66,640] schools, libraries, hospitals, and community-based organizations. The Deaf and Disabled Telecommunications Program mainly  

[15:3:66,650] provides equipment and telephone access services to people with hearing and other disabilities. The California Advanced  

[15:4:66,661] Services Fund Program mainly encourages the deployment of broadband infrastructure in unserved and underserved rural and  

[15:5:66,671] urban areas of California. The California High Cost Fund Programs provide subsidies to carriers obligated to provide telephone  

[15:6:66,681] service to customers residing in rural and high-cost areas.  

[16:0:66,701] 6690 - REGULATION OF TRANSPORTATION 

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[1:0:416,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — GG   45 

[2:0:173,32] 8660   Public Utilities Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The PUC oversees the safety of railroads, rail transit systems, rail crossings, and other forms of motor conveyance in  

[3:1:66,79] California. All railroads, seven major rail transit agencies, six smaller transit systems, and more than 13,500 public and private  

[3:2:66,89] crossings are subject to PUC regulation and inspection. For traditional heavy rail, the PUC's specially trained and federally-

[3:3:66,99] certified inspectors inspect all tracks (annually), all train equipment and facilities (thrice annually), and all signals and  

[3:4:66,109] appurtenances; investigate all rail accidents resulting in loss of life and property damage; and ensure safe transport of  

[3:5:66,120] hazardous materials by rail.  

[4:0:66,139] For rail transit systems, PUC inspectors and engineers conduct safety inspections of track, vehicles, signals and train control,  

[4:1:66,150] and operations, as well as overall agency triennial safety audits. The PUC also has exclusive authority to approve or  

[4:2:66,160] disapprove all highway-rail crossings of railroad or rail transit tracks, as well as crossing safety treatments deployed at each  

[4:3:66,170] crossing. Additionally, the PUC jointly (with Caltrans) administers the state's annual allocation of federal funds for crossing  

[4:4:66,180] hazard elimination projects and the state's grade separation program, which assists localities with the high cost of grade-

[4:5:66,190] separating highway/rail crossings.  

[5:0:66,210] The transportation program also oversees passenger carriers, including privately-owned for-hire passenger transportation  

[5:1:66,220] companies (e.g., limousines, airport shuttles, transportation network companies, and charter and scheduled bus operators) and  

[5:2:66,231] privately-owned water ferries. The program regulates rates and services, issues certificates and other licenses, enforces safety  

[5:3:66,241] standards and insurance requirements, and protects consumers from for-hire passenger carriers operating illegally.  

[6:0:66,261] 6695 - PUBLIC ADVOCATE'S OFFICE  

[7:0:66,278] The mandate and function of the Public Advocate's Office is defined in Public Utilities Code Section 309.5. The Public  

[7:1:66,289] Advocate's Office is a statutorily-defined state entity charged with representing and advocating on behalf of public utility  

[7:2:66,299] customers and subscribers in all significant proceedings within the PUC's jurisdiction.  

[8:0:66,316] Chapter 856, Statutes of 1996 (SB 960), codified the Public Advocate's Office with a director appointed by and serving at the  

[8:1:66,327] pleasure of the Governor and charged the Public Advocate's Office with representing ratepayer interests in PUC proceedings.  

[8:2:66,337] Chapter 1005, Statutes of 1999 (AB 1658), directed funds from the Public Utilities Commission Utilities Reimbursement  

[8:3:66,347] Account to be transferred in the annual budget act to the Public Utilities Commission Public Advocate's Office Account. The law  

[8:4:66,357] further required that "funds in the Public Utilities Commission Public Advocate's Office Account shall be utilized exclusively by  

[8:5:66,367] the division in the performance of its duties as determined by the director" of the Public Advocates Office.  

[8:6:66,385] Chapter 440, Statutes of 2001 (SB 201), removed the limitation that the Public Advocate's Office only represent ratepayers in  

[8:7:66,395] PUC proceedings. SB 201 extended the Public Advocate's Office representation to other venues such as, but not limited to, the  

[8:8:66,406] Energy Commission, Air Resources Board, California Independent System Operator, and legislative hearings.

[10:0:72,535] 8780   Milton Marks "Little Hoover" Commission on California State Governmental  

[10:1:228,550] Organization and Economy  

[11:0:66,571] The Milton Marks "Little Hoover" Commission on California State Government Organization and Economy (Commission) is the  

[11:1:66,581] state's only independent and citizen-based oversight mechanism. It conducts four to five comprehensive reviews of executive  

[11:2:66,592] branch programs, departments, and agencies each year and recommends ways to improve performance by increasing  

[11:3:66,602] efficiency and effectiveness with existing resources. The Commission is statutorily responsible for analyzing and making  

[11:4:66,612] recommendations to the Legislature on all Governor reorganization plans.  

[12:0:66,632] The Commission is composed of two members of the Senate, two members of the Assembly, and nine unpaid citizen members  

[12:1:66,643] - five appointed by the Governor and four appointed by the Legislature.  

[13:0:66,663] Because of the Commission's independence, the Legislature charged it with oversight of the California State Auditor, including  

[13:1:66,673] contracting with an independent auditor for annually examining the State Audit Fund.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[12:4:66,641] but are not limited to, examining any laws, practices, or conditions concerning or affecting women and girls which impose  

[12:5:66,652] special limitations or burdens upon them or upon society, or which limit or tend to limit opportunities available to women and  

[12:6:66,662] girls generally and specifically as it relates to gender equity in the media, educational needs of women and girls, gender in the  

[12:7:66,672] workplace and employment, equal pay for women, health and safety of women and girls, and women in the military, women

[9:0:182,512] 8830   California Law Revision Commission  

[9:1:66,533] The California Law Revision Commission (Commission) has the responsibility to make a continuing substantive review of  

[9:2:66,543] California statutory and decisional law, to recommend legislation to make needed reforms, and to make recommendations to  

[9:3:66,554] the Governor and Legislature for revision of the law on major topics (as assigned by the Legislature) that require detailed study  

[9:4:66,564] and cannot easily be handled in the ordinary legislative process.  

[10:0:66,584] The Commission consists of seven gubernatorial appointees plus one Senator, one Assembly Member, and the Legislative  

[10:1:66,594] Counsel. The Commission's work is independent, nonpartisan, and objective.  

[10:2:66,612] The Commission also administers and provides staff support for the Committee on Revision of the Penal Code. The Committee  

[10:3:66,622] is responsible for studying the Penal Code and   recommending reforms to simplify and rationalize California’s criminal law and  

[10:4:66,632] procedures.   The Committee consists of five gubernatorial appointees, one Senator, and one Assembly Member.

[13:0:197,655] 8855   California State Auditor's Office  

[13:1:66,677] The California State Auditor (State Auditor) promotes the effective and efficient administration and management of public funds  

[13:2:66,687] and programs by providing citizens and government officials nonpartisan, accurate, and objective assessments of state and  

[13:3:66,697] local governments' financial and operational activities. As the independent auditor, the state law exclusively grants the State  

[13:4:66,707] Auditor full access to all records of state and local agencies, special districts, school districts, and any publicly-created entity.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 72 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — GG   70 

[1:0:446,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[2:0:162,32] 8855   California State Auditor's Office - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The State Auditor conducts performance, financial, or compliance audits that are either mandated by statute or requested by  

[3:1:66,79] the Legislature through the Joint Legislative Audit Committee (JLAC). Further, the State Auditor is responsible for annually  

[3:2:66,89] conducting California's statewide Single Audit - a combination of the independent audit of the State's basic financial statements  

[3:3:66,99] and the independent audit of numerous federal programs administered by California. The Single Audit is federally required as a  

[3:4:66,109] condition for California to receive billions in federal funds each year. In 2011-12, the Legislature enacted a program that  

[3:5:66,120] requires the State Auditor to conduct pilot audits of the procurement practices of six trial courts in 2012-13 and thereafter  

[3:6:66,130] biennially audit the procurement practices of the Administrative Office of the Courts and five judicial branch entities - which  

[3:7:66,140] includes trial courts, Appellate Courts, and the Habeas Corpus Resource Center.  

[4:0:66,160] The State Auditor also identifies statewide issues or specific government entities as being at-high risk for waste, fraud, abuse,  

[4:1:66,171] and mismanagement or that have major challenges related to efficiency and effectiveness. The State Auditor has the authority  

[4:2:66,181] to conduct evaluations of those issues and entities identified as being high risk. Similar to the state high-risk program, Chapter  

[4:3:66,191] 451, Statutes of 2011 (AB 187), also authorizes the State Auditor, to the extent resources are available, to identify local  

[4:4:66,201] government agencies - including city, county, or special districts or publicly-created entities - as being at-high risk for the  

[4:5:66,211] potential of waste, fraud, abuse, or mismanagement or that have major challenges associated with their economy, efficiency, or  

[4:6:66,222] effectiveness. Further, if approved by the JLAC, the State Auditor can audit these identified local entities.  

[5:0:66,242] Additionally, under the California Whistleblower Protection Act, the State Auditor's office has broad authority to perform  

[5:1:66,252] independent investigations into allegations of improper governmental activities by employees at state agencies and courts. The  

[5:2:66,262] State Auditor's office administers California's Whistleblower Hotline that enables the public to report allegations of improper  

[5:3:66,273] acts committed by the courts, state agencies, departments, or employees, as defined by law.  

[6:0:66,293] The Voters FIRST Act, approved by voters in November 2008 as Proposition 11, requires the State Auditor to initiate an  

[6:1:66,303] application process for selecting the members of a 14-member Citizens Redistricting Commission (Commission) every 10  

[6:2:66,313] years. The State Auditor develops regulations to implement the application and selection process of the Commission and  

[6:3:66,324] conducts comprehensive outreach to prospective applicants to serve on the Commission.

[18:1:66,670] 6770 - STATE BUDGET  

[19:0:66,691] To ensure the financial integrity of the state through the planned allocation of state resources, the Department of Finance  

[19:1:66,701] advises the Governor on the fiscal condition of the state and prepares the annual Governor's Budget. The Governor's Budget  

[19:2:66,711] contains past year actual expenditures and revenues, current year estimated expenditures and revenues, and proposed

[2:0:187,32] 8860   Department of Finance - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] expenditures and estimated revenues for the next fiscal year. After the budget is enacted, the Department of Finance assists in  

[3:1:66,79] the administration of the budget. The Department of Finance also conducts performance reviews of state agencies and  

[3:2:66,89] programs, recommends ways to lower the costs of state government and to better serve the public within existing resources,  

[3:3:66,99] and provides fiscal analyses of bills introduced in the Legislature and makes recommendations to the Governor. Basic and  

[3:4:66,109] applied economic and tax research functions support the revenue estimates required for the development of the state financial  

[3:5:66,120] plan, for the analysis of financial legislation, and the evaluation of economic trends or other fiscal developments which affect  

[3:6:66,130] the state. Basic and applied demographic research functions provide data to support the workload estimates of state agencies.  

[3:7:66,148] 6775 - FINANCIAL INFORMATION SYSTEM FOR CALIFORNIA (FI$Cal) PROJECT SUPPORT  

[4:0:66,168] The Department of Finance is one of four partner agencies responsible for the development, implementation, utilization, and  

[4:1:66,178] maintenance of an integrated statewide financial and administrative information technology system, FI$Cal. This program  

[4:2:66,188] includes Department of Finance subject-matter experts assigned to the FI$Cal Project.  

[4:3:66,206] 6780 - STATE AUDITS AND EVALUATIONS  

[5:0:66,226] The Department of Finance systematically reviews and evaluates state-administered and financed programs. The Office of  

[5:1:66,237] State Audits and Evaluations assists the Director of Finance in fulfilling the statutory responsibilities for supervision over all  

[5:2:66,247] matters concerning the financial and business policies of the state by conducting financial audits and by performing critical  

[5:3:66,257] examinations of state programs and policies including the oversight activities associated with the dissolution of redevelopment  

[5:4:66,267] agencies.  

[6:0:66,285] 6785 - STATEWIDE ACCOUNTING POLICIES, CONSULTING AND TRAINING  

[7:0:66,305] The Department of Finance is responsible for devising and maintaining a modern and uniform system of statewide accounting. 

[7:1:66,316] The Fiscal Systems and Consulting Unit sets statewide fiscal and accounting policies and procedures.   The unit provides fiscal  

[7:2:66,326] and accounting training, advice, and consulting services to departments to ensure that the state's assets are protected, and  

[7:3:66,336] that   accurate and timely financial information is maintained.   The Department of Finance participates in the development of  

[7:4:66,346] statewide financial systems.

[7:0:164,378] 8880   Financial Information System for California  

[8:0:66,399] The Department of FI$Cal operates the statewide financial system to enable California to perform budgeting, accounting,  

[8:1:66,410] procurement and cash management functions transparently and efficiently. The Department of FI$Cal is responsible for  

[8:2:66,420] supporting the Financial Information System for California (FI$Cal) Project, including supporting the system's customers and  

[8:3:66,430] stakeholders.  

[9:0:66,450] The FI$Cal Project is a partnership of four control agencies: the Department of Finance, the State Controller's Office, the State  

[9:1:66,461] Treasurer's Office, and the Department of General Services. FI$Cal provides the state with a single integrated financial  

[9:2:66,471] management system that encompasses budgeting, accounting, procurement, cash management, and financial management  

[9:3:66,481] and reporting. FI$Cal, through the adoption of best business practices, reengineers business processes; improves efficiency;  

[9:4:66,491] enhances decision making and resource management; and provides reliable, accessible, and timely statewide financial  

[9:5:66,501] information allowing the state to be more transparent.   FI$Cal further enables transparency through its Open FI$Cal website,  

[9:6:66,512] which was implemented in 2018 pursuant to Government Code section 11862.   The website will continue to expand providing  

[9:7:66,522] the public with access to a growing number of the state's non-confidential expenditures.

[8:0:196,291] 8885   Commission on State Mandates  

[8:1:66,313] The Commission on State Mandates timely renders sound, quasi-judicial decisions, in compliance with Article XIII B, Section 6  

[8:2:66,323] of the California Constitution, resolving disputes regarding reimbursement for state-mandated local programs and relieving  

[8:3:66,333] unnecessary congestion of the courts. Its mission is to fairly and impartially hear and determine matters filed by state and local  

[8:4:66,343] government; resolve complex legal questions in a deliberative and timely manner; and produce clear, well-reasoned, and lawful  

[8:5:66,353] decisions.  

[9:0:66,371] The Commission is a quasi-judicial body consisting of the Director of Finance, the State Controller, the State Treasurer, the  

[9:1:66,381] Director of the Office of Planning and Research, a public member with experience in public finance, and two members from the  

[9:2:66,392] categories of city council member, county supervisor, or school district governing board member, appointed by the Governor  

[9:3:66,402] and approved by the Senate.

[8:0:231,369] 8940   Military Department  

[9:0:66,390] The Military Department is responsible for the command, leadership and management of the California National Guard, Youth  

[9:1:66,401] and Community Programs, State Guard and the Naval Militia. The California Military Department, under proper authority,  

[9:2:66,411] organizes, resources, and trains forces with unique capabilities to serve the community, state, and nation. The purpose of the  

[9:3:66,421] California National Guard is to provide mission ready forces to the federal government as directed by the President, emergency  

[9:4:66,431] public safety support to civil authorities as directed by the Governor, and support to our member's families and to the  

[10:0:66,441] community. With an authorized strength of 19,000, the Army National Guard and Air National Guard are organized, manned,  

[10:1:66,452] and funded in accordance with federal Departments of the Army and Air Force regulatory guidance.  

[11:0:68,469] The Military Department Youth and Community Program serves California communities and families by delivering national  

[11:1:66,480] level, high quality educational support programs, in partnership with the educational community, within a military, academic  

[11:2:66,490] structured environment. In addition to the funding that flows through the State Treasury, the Military Department also receives  

[11:3:66,500] federal funding directly from the Department of Defense. A special display titled "Other Federal Funds" shows the funding  

[11:4:66,510] received from this source.  

[12:0:68,528] Because the Military Department's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay  

[12:1:66,538] program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[9:1:65,380] • Emergency Preparedness and Disaster Response — The Budget includes $2.5 million ongoing General Fund to address the  

[9:2:76,391] increased magnitude, frequency, and complexity of natural disasters. In recent years, the California Military Department has

[9:4:76,411] recovery. These resources will enhance current logistical, administrative, and fiscal support operations, as well as future  

[9:5:76,421] planning and training to prepare for contingencies as the nature of disasters and emergencies evolve.

[4:0:66,308] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:0:66,327] 6911 - NATIONAL GUARD  

[6:0:66,355] The Military Department will maximize the readiness of the California National Guard's Soldiers and Airmen, along with our  

[6:1:66,365] State Guard. Army National Guard support plans include a community-based land force, logistics, communications, law  

[7:0:66,376] enforcement and other specialized support. Air National Guard support plans include rescue, air defense, airlift and unmanned  

[7:1:66,386] aerial systems, space, intelligence, communications, and other specialized services. The Office of the Adjutant General  

[7:2:66,396] element governs the joint activities and performance of the Military Department in areas such as personnel and fiscal resource  

[7:3:66,406] management, judicial affairs, internal controls, facility management, and information technology. The Military Civil Support  

[7:4:66,416] element provides liaison and coordination with federal, state, and local agencies so that mutual understanding and unity of  

[7:5:66,427] purpose is assured during an emergency. The objectives of this element are to plan, prepare, and train for the deployment of  

[7:6:66,437] Military Department personnel and equipment to support civil authority when called to state service by the Governor due to  

[7:7:66,447] domestic emergency or natural disaster and to provide the state, county, city, and other public agencies with the coordination  

[7:8:66,457] necessary to ensure a timely, organized response. The State Guard is a volunteer component of the Military Department whose  

[7:9:66,467] mission is to provide a trained, disciplined and ready force during training, preparation for mobilization, demobilization, and  

[7:10:66,478] provision of support to civil authorities during periods of state emergencies. The federal government provides virtually all  

[7:11:66,488] supplies, equipment, transportation, subsistence, and support services necessary for training, deployment, and commitment of  

[7:12:66,498] the National Guard. Training is conducted in accordance with the Department of the Army and Air Force Regulations and  

[7:13:66,508] Training Guidance.  

[8:0:66,526] 6912 - YOUTH AND COMMUNITY PROGRAMS  

[9:0:66,544] The Military Department manages and maintains partnerships with the following ten programs while serving more than 13,000  

[9:1:66,554] youth annually: California Cadet Corps, Oakland Military Institute, California Military Institute, Porterville Military Academy,  

[9:2:66,564] Grizzly Youth Academy, Sunburst Youth Academy, Discovery ChalleNGe Academy, California JobsChallenge, STARBASE  

[9:3:66,574] Academy Sacramento and STARBASE Academy Los Alamitos. The California Cadet Corps, founded in 1911 by the California  

[9:4:66,584] Legislature and then- Adjutant General BG Edwin Forbes, is a school-based, applied leadership program that is designed to  

[9:5:66,595] provide maximum growth and leadership opportunities for cadets from elementary through high school levels. It provides  

[9:6:66,605] leadership opportunities for cadets by allowing them to conduct training for junior cadets, perform as leaders in their cadet  

[9:7:66,615] military units, and by demonstrating proper behavior and citizenship at their schools and in their communities. As part of their  

[9:8:66,625] training, cadets provide assistance and support to the school and community. The Oakland Military Institute, California Military  

[9:9:66,635] Institute, and Porterville Military Academy develops leaders of character by providing rigorous programs to promote excellence  

[9:10:66,646] in academics, leadership, citizenship, athletics, and physical fitness/wellbeing. Additionally, the programs instill the 10 Cadet  

[9:11:66,656] Corps values of loyalty, education, ambition, duty, enthusiasm, respect, service, health, integrity, and personal courage. Using a  

[9:12:66,666] military framework, these programs graduate cadets who are capable of meeting the admissions requirements for college and  

[9:13:66,676] who are prepared for their roles as future leaders. The mission of the National Guard Youth ChalleNGe Program is to intervene  

[9:14:66,686] in and reclaim the lives of 16-18 year old high school dropouts. Graduates leave the program with the values, life skills,  

[10:0:66,697] education, and self-discipline necessary to succeed as productive citizens. California Jobs ChalleNGe Located at Los Alamitos,  

[10:1:66,707] CA is a continuation program to provide vocational skills to California ChalleNGe graduates.   The overall mission provides  

[11:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] post-secondary Career Technical Education (CTE) training to place graduates into viable jobs. The goal of the Department of  

[3:1:66,79] Defense STARBASE program is to motivate elementary school students, primarily 5th graders, to explore Science, Technology,  

[3:2:66,89] Engineering and Math (STEM) as they continue their education. The curriculum is designed to increase student involvement  

[3:3:66,99] and interest in STEM, enhance their understanding of the role that STEM literacy plays in their lives, strengthen potential for  

[3:4:66,109] future careers, and make the pursuit of STEM activities more attractive and accessible. While attending STARBASE students  

[3:5:66,120] interact with military personnel to explore careers and observe STEM applications in the "real world." These ten youth  

[3:6:66,130] programs are financed with federal, state and local funds. The California National Guard is involved in youth programs because  

[3:7:66,140] political and community leaders at the federal, state, and local levels recognize that the National Guard brings structure,  

[3:8:66,150] discipline, and effective leadership training methods to the educational setting.

[4:0:66,131] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[5:0:66,150] The Army National Guard statewide facilities footprint includes 95 active armories comprised over 2 million square footage. In  

[5:1:66,161] addition, the Military Department possesses 4 aviation centers, 23 field maintenance shops, 2 combined support maintenance  

[5:2:66,171] shops, and 2 maneuver area training equipment sites. The total real property assets of the Military Department encompass an  

[5:3:66,181] area of 7.8 million square feet. The facilities are used to house and train the California National Guard and provide emergency  

[5:4:66,191] public safety support. The Military Department also operates three major training facilities comprised of a total combined  

[5:5:66,201] square footage of 3.4 million. These facilities consist of troop lodging, administration, warehouse, maintenance, firing ranges,  

[5:6:66,212] and maneuver training areas.   Additionally, the Military Department's footprint includes 45 active buildings of 229,000 square  

[5:7:66,222] feet for the Department’s Youth and Community Programs.

[9:0:199,519] 8955   Department of Veterans Affairs  

[10:0:66,540] The California Department of Veterans Affairs (CalVet) honors and serves California's veterans and their families by ensuring  

[10:1:66,550] our veterans have access to the benefits they have earned to enable them to meet their needs. The CalVet promotes and  

[10:2:66,561] delivers innovative services through efficient, cost-effective programs and collaboration with key stakeholders and partners.  

[11:0:66,581] More specifically, the CalVet:  

[11:1:65,599] • Communicates with California's veterans and their families at all pivotal moments throughout their post-military lives to  

[11:2:76,609] educate them and connect them to their federal, state, and local benefits.  

[11:3:65,625] • Provides California veterans and their families with direct representation in presenting their claims and appeals for benefits  

[11:4:76,635] before the US Department of Veterans Affairs and the federal Board of Veteran Appeals.  

[11:5:65,651] • Provides California veterans the opportunity to achieve the American dream of homeownership through direct low-cost loans  

[11:6:76,662] to acquire farms and homes.  

[12:0:65,678] • Provides the state's aged or disabled veterans with premier rehabilitative, residential, and medical care and services in a  

[12:1:76,688] home-like environment at the California Veterans Homes.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:3:66,109] military service.

[5:0:66,157] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[6:0:65,176] • Supportive Services for Formerly Homeless Veterans—The Budget includes $25 million one-time General Fund in 2021-22  

[6:1:76,186] for the Department of Veterans Affairs to administer a competitive grant program to support aging veterans and veterans with  

[6:2:76,196] disabilities who have experienced chronic homelessness. This program will provide a higher level of on-site supportive  

[6:3:76,207] services, such as in-home support to help with habitability, peer specialists to encourage veterans to engage in mental health  

[6:4:76,217] care, and geriatric social workers to identify behavioral issues related to early onset dementia or similar cognitive issues.  

[6:5:65,233] • West Los Angeles Medical Center Housing for Homeless Veterans—The Budget includes $20 million one-time General Fund  

[6:6:76,243] in 2021-22 for the Department of Veterans Affairs to provide resources to the West Los Angeles Veterans Collective to assist  

[6:7:76,253] in building affordable housing units and related infrastructure as part of the permanent supportive housing community on the  

[6:8:76,264] West Los Angeles Veterans Administration Medical Center campus.  

[6:9:65,280] • County Veteran Service Officers—The Budget includes $5.4 million ongoing General Fund to provide additional funding to  

[6:10:76,290] County Veteran Service Officers (CVSO). This brings the total amount of state funding available to CVSOs to $11 million  

[6:11:76,300] annually.

[4:0:66,277] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[4:1:66,296] 6990 - FARM AND HOME LOANS TO VETERANS  

[5:0:66,317] The CalVet Home Loan Program offers veterans, meeting specified requirements, loans to purchase, construct, or rehabilitate  

[5:1:66,327] new or existing single family dwellings, which include condominiums, units in shared equity cooperative housing developments,  

[5:2:66,337] mobile homes, and farms. The CalVet also offers Home Improvement Loans to active CalVet customers or homeowners who  

[5:3:66,347] own their homes free of debt.  

[5:4:66,365] 6995 - VETERANS CLAIMS AND RIGHTS  

[6:0:66,386] The Veteran Services Division provides service and assistance to California's veterans, dependents, and survivors. Programs  

[6:1:66,396] administered consist of: Veterans Dependents Educational Assistance Program, County Veterans Service Office Program,  

[6:2:66,406] Veterans Outreach Program, California Transition Assistance Program, Veterans Housing and Homelessness Prevention  

[6:3:66,416] program, Medi-Cal Cost Avoidance Program, Veterans Claims and Appeals Representation, State Veteran Cemeteries,  

[6:4:66,426] California State Approving Agency for Veterans Education, Disabled Veteran Business Enterprise Program, Veterans Mental  

[6:5:66,437] Health Program including Veteran Justice Outreach and Incarcerated Veterans, Homeless Veterans Outreach Program,  

[6:6:66,447] Veterans Driver License Program, and the Veterans License Plate Program. California, with approximately 1.6 million veterans,  

[6:7:66,457] represents 8.4 percent of the nation's total veteran population.  

[6:8:66,475] 7000 - CARE OF SICK AND DISABLED VETERANS  

[7:0:66,495] CalVet operates eight licensed long-term care facilities which provide affordable, quality services to eligible veterans and  

[7:1:66,505] spouses. The Veterans Homes also provide therapeutic care and rehabilitative services to help   sustain activities of daily living  

[7:2:66,515] and independent function. The primary services provided by these Veterans Homes are:  

[7:3:65,533] • The Veterans Home of California, Barstow offers skilled nursing, intermediate, and domiciliary care.  

[7:4:65,549] • The Veterans Home of California, Chula Vista offers skilled nursing, assisted living, and domiciliary care.  

[7:5:65,566] • The Veterans Home of California, Fresno offers skilled nursing (including memory care) and assisted living care.  

[7:6:65,582] • The Veterans Home of California, Greater Los Angeles and Ventura County (GLAVC), includes three main campuses: West  

[7:7:76,592] Los Angeles (WLA), Lancaster, and Ventura. All three offer assisted living care. The WLA campus also offers skilled nursing  

[7:8:76,602] care (including memory care).  

[8:0:65,618] • The Veterans Home of California, Redding offers skilled nursing (including memory care) and assisted living care.  

[8:1:65,635] • The Veterans Home of California, Yountville is the largest veterans home in the United States. It offers all four levels of care:  

[8:2:76,645] skilled nursing (including memory care), intermediate, assisted living, and domiciliary care.  

[8:3:66,662] 7005 - VETERANS MEMORIALS  

[9:0:66,680] This program supports the Veterans Registry, which is part of the California Veterans Memorial. The provided contributions help  

[9:1:66,690] to defray the costs of data entry and system management for the Registry and the reasonable costs that are incurred by the  

[9:2:66,701] CalVet for administering the fund.  

[10:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[14:0:66,663] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[15:0:66,682] The Department of Veterans Affairs (CalVet) facilities include eight veteran’s homes, three state veterans’ cemeteries, and a  

[15:1:66,692] headquarters office building. The eight veteran’s homes are located in Yountville, Barstow, Chula Vista, West Los Angeles,  

[15:2:66,703] Lancaster, Ventura, Redding, and Fresno on 766 acres of land, with 2.4 million gross square feet of building space.  

[15:3:66,713] Additionally, CalVet will construct a new 285,000 square foot Skill Nursing Facility in Yountville, California and will expand  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] columbarium capacity at the Northern California Veterans Cemetery in Igo, California. Veteran’s homes provide domiciliary  

[3:1:66,79] housing for the state's veterans and are also licensed to provide three levels of care, ranging from assisted living to skilled  

[3:2:66,89] nursing. CalVet also operates three cemeteries that are located in Igo, near Redding, Yountville, and Seaside and contain a  

[3:3:66,99] total of approximately 24,000 gravesites on 47 developed acres.

[11:0:66,498] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:1:66,517] 7500 - HOMEOWNERS' PROPERTY TAX RELIEF  

[12:0:66,538] In order to reduce California taxpayers' property tax burden, the California Constitution exempts homeowners from paying  

[12:1:66,548] property taxes on the first $7,000 of the assessed value of their principal place of residence. The Constitution requires the state  

[12:2:66,558] to reimburse local governments for revenues lost due to the homeowners' exemption.   This Program also receives repayments  

[12:3:66,568] from persons participating in the Senior Citizens and Disabled Citizens Property Tax Postponement Program, whereby the  

[12:4:66,578] state makes property tax payments for qualified homeowners in exchange for the participants making repayment when they  

[12:5:66,589] relinquish ownership.  

[12:6:66,606] 7505 - SUBVENTIONS FOR OPEN SPACE  

[13:0:66,627] The Williamson Act permits cities and counties to enter into contracts with landowners to limit the use of land to agricultural,  

[13:1:66,637] scenic, and open space purposes. Article XIII, section 8, of the California Constitution permits land under such restrictions to be  

[13:2:66,647] assessed at other than market value based on its limited uses. The state provides reimbursements to cities and counties to  

[13:3:66,657] partially defray the loss of property tax revenues. The Department of Conservation administers this program.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[11:0:171,538] 9285   Trial Court Security - Court Construction  

[12:0:66,559] This budget establishes a process and mechanism for counties to request funding if court construction projects, occupied on or  

[12:1:66,569] after October 9, 2011, modify or create building features that increase overall trial court security costs. Trial Court Security was

[12:3:66,590] requires the state to provide annual funding for newly required activities that have an overall effect of increasing county costs in  

[12:4:66,600] this realigned program.

[6:5:66,495] Federal Receipts from Flood Control Lands-Monies received by the federal government for lands acquired for flood protection  

[6:6:66,505] purposes are prorated to the counties in which the lands are located.  

[6:7:66,523] Federal Receipts from Forest Reserves-Monies received by the federal government for the state's share of receipts from forest  

[6:8:66,533] reserves are prorated to counties where the lands are located.  

[6:9:66,551] Federal Receipts from Grazing Land Fees-Monies received by the federal government for the state's share of receipts from  

[6:10:66,561] grazing land are prorated to counties where the lands are located.  

[6:11:66,579] Federal Receipts from Potash Leases-Monies received by the federal government for the state's share of potash leases are  

[6:12:66,589] distributed to school districts.

[10:0:171,488] 9620   Cash Management and Budgetary Loans  

[10:1:66,509] The Cash Management and Budgetary Loans budget tracks General Fund expenditures for interest costs related to cash flow  

[10:2:66,520] and budgetary borrowing. Internal and external cash flow borrowing can be used to overcome normal cash flow imbalances  

[10:3:66,530] during the fiscal year. Because receipts and disbursements occur unevenly throughout the fiscal year, the General Fund may  

[10:4:66,540] need to borrow even though its budget is balanced. Budgetary loans may be used to loan monies from various state funds to  

[10:5:66,550] the General Fund to help bridge a budget shortfall. These loans are temporary, and do not harm the operations of departmental  

[10:6:66,560] programs.

[10:0:66,362] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:0:66,382] 7720 - CASH MANAGEMENT  

[12:0:66,402] Borrowing From Other Funds or Other State/Local Entities  

[13:0:66,422] Chapter 312, Statutes of 1907, authorized the transfers to the General Fund from certain specified funds (often referred to as  

[13:1:66,433] "borrowable" funds) in the State Treasury System whenever the General Fund was exhausted. Those provisions, in slightly  

[13:2:66,443] modified form, are now contained in Sections 16310 and 16418 of the Government Code. This authorization enables the  

[13:3:66,453] General Fund to overcome normal cash flow imbalances throughout the fiscal year to a large extent. The use of these funds'  

[13:4:66,463] money for General Fund cash flow purposes is largely transparent to most of these funds. This mechanism does not interfere  

[13:5:66,473] with the day-to-day cash needs of the borrowable funds because of the implementation method. Generally, when a fund is  

[13:6:66,484] designated as borrowable for General Fund cash flow purposes, it merely increases the amount of cash the State Controller's  

[13:7:66,494] Office is authorized to borrow from the Pooled Money Investment Account (PMIA) when there is a General Fund cash  

[13:8:66,504] shortage. For most of these funds, actual cash is not transferred out of the funds into the General Fund. The entire cash  

[13:9:66,514] balances of these funds remain intact and completely available for the funds' purposes.  

[14:0:66,535] A State Agency Investment Fund (SAIF) Program was enacted pursuant to Chapter 142, Statutes of 2011 (Senate Bill 79,  

[14:1:66,545] Government Code section 16330), to increase internal borrowable resources through the receipt of deposits from a state entity  

[14:2:66,555] that is not required to deposit or invest funds in the PMIA. The minimum deposit or investment per participant is $500 million  

[14:3:66,565] and the maximum amount for the SAIF Program for all participants is $10 billion. There are currently no deposits in this  

[14:4:66,575] Program.  

[15:0:66,596] A Voluntary Investment Program (VIP) was enacted pursuant to Chapter 44, Statutes of 2012 (Senate Bill 1033, Government  

[15:1:66,606] Code section 16340), for the receipt of voluntary deposits from local entities for the purpose of providing additional liquidity for  

[15:2:66,616] the state's cash management. The minimum deposit from a local entity is $200 million and the maximum amount for the VIP  

[15:3:66,626] from all local entities combined is $10 billion. No deposits have been made into this Program.  

[16:0:66,657] Borrowing From Financial Markets  

[17:0:66,677] In addition, the courts have validated temporary external borrowing that meets the "appropriation doctrine." Under this doctrine,  

[17:1:66,688] an obligation is not considered a debt or liability within the State Constitutional limitation on indebtedness (Section 1 of Article  

[17:2:66,698] XVI) if an appropriation is made from existing funds or reasonably anticipated funds subject to appropriation. This doctrine was  

[17:3:66,708] invoked in 1933 and 1936 to uphold the use of registered warrants (IOUs) during the Great Depression and again in 1971 to  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:446,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[2:0:136,32] 9620   Cash Management and Budgetary Loans - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] validate the State of California Notes provisions of Chapter 223, Statutes of 1971. The Notes provisions were invoked in  

[3:1:66,79] 1971-72 in lieu of the more costly registered warrant authority used during the Depression and, like registered warrants,  

[3:2:66,89] required the projected exhaustion of all internal sources of funds before being implemented. The Notes provisions were re-

[3:3:66,99] enacted by Chapter 10X, Statutes of 1983, and employed in 1982-83 and 1983-84.  

[4:0:66,120] In 1984-85, the state implemented a cash management program pursuant to Chapter 268, Statutes of 1984, which provided the  

[4:1:66,130] flexibility to borrow from external sources prior to exhausting internal sources. Under this program, the use of external funds  

[4:2:66,140] results in potential savings to the General Fund, as well as increased revenue to the various special funds from which the  

[4:3:66,150] General Fund would have borrowed from pursuant to Sections 16310 and 16418 of the Government Code.  

[5:0:66,171] External sources of borrowing available to the state include revenue anticipation notes (RANs), revenue anticipation warrants  

[5:1:66,181] (RAWs), and registered warrants (short-term IOUs). RANs and RAWs are short-term debt obligations issued in anticipation of  

[5:2:66,191] receiving revenues in the near future.  

[6:0:66,211] Additional costs to the cash management program include, but are not limited to, costs to issue and redeem IOUs, and any  

[6:1:66,222] other program measures necessary to effectively manage cash flow.  

[7:0:66,252] Overall Program  

[8:0:66,273] Pursuant to Government Code Sections 12020 and 12021, accounts payable and receivable and cash flow statements for the  

[8:1:66,283] past, current, and budget years are included as Schedule 5 in the Governor's Budget Summary. Neither cash receipts nor  

[8:2:66,293] disbursements match revenues and expenditures presented elsewhere in this Budget. To reflect cash flow, budget data is  

[8:3:66,303] converted to cash basis primarily by adjusting for cash collected by an agency but not yet recorded by the Controller and for  

[8:4:66,313] accruals. Adjustments are also made to reflect statutory changes, anticipated legislative actions, and administrative actions.  

[9:0:66,334] The estimated current and budget year cash flows incorporate the latest revenue and expenditure budget measures and  

[9:1:66,344] assumptions. During difficult cash periods, cash management measures such as intra-year payment deferrals may be used  

[9:2:66,354] from time-to-time to address low points during the fiscal year.  

[9:3:66,372] 7725 - BUDGETARY LOANS  

[10:0:66,390] The Budget Act authorizes budgetary loans from various funds and accounts to the General Fund. These loans are repaid over  

[10:1:66,400] multiple fiscal years. Unless otherwise specified in statute, the loans are repaid with interest calculated at the Pooled Money  

[10:2:66,410] Investment Account rate on the date of transfer.

[8:0:157,323] 9625   Interest Payments to the Federal Government  

[9:0:66,344] Pursuant to the Cash Management Improvement Act of 1990 (CMIA), the state will incur an interest liability to the federal  

[9:1:66,355] government. The purpose of CMIA is to ensure greater efficiency, effectiveness, and equity in the exchange of funds between  

[9:2:66,365] the federal government and the state. The major provisions of CMIA are: (a) federal agencies must make timely fund  

[9:3:66,375] disbursements and grant awards to the state; (b) the state must minimize the time between the deposit of federal funds in the  

[9:4:66,385] state account and the payout of the funds for program purposes; (c) the state is entitled to interest from the federal government  

[9:5:66,395] from the time state funds are paid out for program purposes until federal funds are deposited in the state account; and (d) the  

[9:6:66,406] federal government is entitled to interest from the state from the time federal funds are deposited in the state account until the  

[9:7:66,416] funds are paid out for program purposes.  

[10:0:66,436] The federal assistance programs impacted by the CMIA are those programs that have $285 million or more in federal fund  

[10:1:66,446] expenditures. For the majority of these programs, state departments request federal funds in advance of the warrant (i.e.,  

[10:2:66,457] check) issuance. State departments use this funding technique because the State Constitution requires that the funds be  

[10:3:66,467] deposited before the warrants are issued.  

[11:0:66,487] Interest payments to the federal government are due no later than March 31 each year. The payment will be for the interest  

[11:1:66,497] liability incurred during the state's prior fiscal year.

[14:0:168,635] 9650   Health and Dental Benefits for Annuitants  

[15:0:66,656] This program provides funding for health and dental benefit services for retired state employees and their dependents.  

[16:0:66,676] The program began on January 1, 1962, with an employer contribution of $5 per month toward the cost of a basic health plan.  

[16:1:66,687] Since then, major medical plans, Medicare, and plans supplementing Medicare have been developed. Dental care was added  

[16:2:66,697] in 1982.
